Texans, and their patron. St. Patrick, in an afternoon of family entertain­ ment.

D isagreements over how to use a proposed city residential growth plan in sewer development planning will be d i s c u s s e d at a s p e c i a l m e e t i n g 
Wednesday.

Zachry, c h a i r m a n of the Areawide

City Planning Commission and now awaiting City Council endorsement. 
In r e c e n t APAC m e e t i n g s , th e growth sketch has been criticized as unduly r e s t r i c t i v e in s o m e a r e a s , especially the North Side.

Basis for planning

Bexar County Medical E xam iner Dr. 
Ruben Santos ruled the death a hom i­ cide He placed the time of death at about I a.m. Feb. 2o and said Zepeda died a l m o s t i n s t a n t l y fro m s e v e r e blows to the abdomen. 
Several jail guards have already ta­ ken poly g rap h e x a m in a tio n s in co n ­ nection with the incident. More tests arc scheduled and none of the results have been released. 
P olice D e p a r t m e n t officia ls also launched a probe into the incident and subsequently the Civil Rights Division asked for the FBI report. 
Sheriff’s Et. Kenneth Bilhartz said 
Monday the investigation has now ex­ tended beyong the original ju r is d ic ­ tional boundaries. 
" W e a re tak in g depositions from people who saw police officers arrest

• Findings of sheriff's investigators. 

• Investigative files from the police department.

‘‘I read your column which referred to McAllister Park as a ‘powder keg' crammed with ‘a real rough rock festi­ val type crowd.” ’ writes a teen-age reader. 

“ I would appreciate you printing th e y o u n g p e o p l e ’s s id e of th e story.... 
“ Ju st b ecau s e som e of us have long hair. drink beer, sm oke pot a n d lis te n to loud ro ck m u s i c doesn't mean we are any wilder or any more violent than older people. 
“ The park, to us, is just a place for seeing our friends, listening to o u r m u s i c , e n j o y i n g th e nice w e a t h e r and g e n e r a l l y h a v i n g a good time. 
Violence 
“ In all Hu* many limes I ve been there, I have never seen any vio- lence. 
“ I c a n ’t understand why people think the younger generation is just a bunch of ‘hooligans,’ as you so graciously titled us ... 
“ What do people think we are — crazy m a n iac s who th reaten e v e r ­ yone who dares interfere with our lives? 
“ All we want is to enjoy a beau­ tiful Sunday with our friends, If people are too scared to be around us. there are plenty of other places to go*...” 
And from an older m e m b e r of the 
Sunday afternoon McAllister P ark set comes this response to the sam e column: 
" I ’m 39, have been m a rr ie d 15 years and have worked for the same company 2o years..,. 
" E v e s e en m o r e t r o u b l e in 
Brackenridge P ark on Sunday than at McAllister.

"Why not leave it the way it is and let us congregate in one place where we can listen to the Eagles, ride our motorcycles and drink our b eer?" 
So th e r e ’s the other side of the 
McAllister Park issue — fur what it s worth.

Matt Garcia and has been political­ ly active in the district for several years.

HUNTSVILLE

Hampton Robinson III of Victoria one year in ja il and a $2,ooo fine following his conviction of negligent homicide in tin1 shooting death of Thomas Bauer, 
32. also of Victoria, on Oct. 9.

 assessing the penalty. The jury had d eliberated 15 m in u tes S atu rd ay before finding Robinson guilty. The prosecution had sought a conviction on a charge of voluntary m anslaughter which carries a stiffer penalty. 
Bauer was shot once in the back of the head outside tilt' apartment of a woman both he and R obinson had 

 dated. The shooting occurred in Vic­ toria. 
Robinson admitted shooting Bauer, but argued that the shooting was ac­ cidental. He said Bauer had threatened him with a gun and he in turn pulled a pistol and his pistol went off when he hit Bauer w ith it while trying lo defend himself.

With absolutely no fan­ fare at all, a revolution is taking place on Main 
Plaza. In a sudden re­ versal of the prevailing downtown policy, a park ing lot is actually being turned into a new build­ ing instead of vice-versa. 
What you are seeing is the next to the last phase of the massive renovation and restoration of San 
Fernando 
Cathedral which is being carried out by the Archdiocese of 
San Antonio. 
As revealed in 
Action-Express early last year, the ultimate plan for the restored cathedral was to remove the priests’ quarters from the rear of the church and expose the apse (that is the rear) and the domed sanctuary which were part of the original 
Spanish church and which are still intact and enclosed inside the pre­ sent residence structure which faces City Hall and 
Military Plaza. 
Archbishop Francis r'urey has decided to go ahead with that work now rather than wait. What is being built on the former parking lot adjacent to the historic cathedral is the new priests’ r e si­ dence. 
The decision was made after AIIRight Parking had just completed a contract tor the property, so not all of the property is available for church use just yet. 
The new residence is being built well hack from Main Plaza, but is low enough and has sim- 

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuetdoy Morch 15 1977 
H 
Poge 
5-A 

 pie architectural features so it will not detract from the cathedral. 
Once the new residence is built, the old residence will be demolished, ex­ posing the original church structure — said by some to be the oldest existing church sane tuary in the 
United 
States. 

The 
San 
Fernando 
Church was used as lookout by Travis's men prior to the arrival of the 
Mexican forces and Santa 
Anna set up his head quarters in front of the cathedral in preparation for the Battle of the Ala mo. At that time, there was nothing hut the river and open country between San Fernando and Mission San Antonio. 
Meanwhile, parking will continue on the lot in front of the new resi donee for a couple of years until the amended contract with the parking operators expires. When the cars are moved away from there, a mini-park ot trees, tiles and, per haps, a small fountain will grace that important downtown corner front­ ing Main Plaza. 
That whole area is tak­ ing on a new look with 
Plaza de Armas coming along on its restoration behind City Hall. This is the old Vogel Belt Build ing and it is being turned into a really splendid restored shopping gal lery. All of this is great news lor the 
Main Military plazas ar ca. 

R e a l p a in tin g s? 

I a m sure everyone has been watching those com mercials on television about the great art on sale here and there with nothing over $2o. 
But we have also seen the letters to Paul T hom p­ son criticizing these art works as “ assembly line” paintings. 
It was never explained what assembly line paint­ ings ate. Are they good. bad. indifferent or what? 
JI ST WONDERING 

The worst cries, of course, are coming from people who have long supported the Starving 
Artists Show of the Little 
Church of La Villita. 
Some other groups have tried to capitalize on the name and the church went to court to get them to stop. So instead of starving artists, we now have 
“ underfed” or 
“ hungry” artists. 

Basically the c o m ­ plaint is that the paint­ ings being sold in these one and two-day exhibi­ tions are not really ori­ ginal art works in the usual sense — or at least not art works like those sold at the Starving Ar­ tlet* Show. 
What they mean by 
“ assembly-line” paint­ ing is that hundreds of canvases are erected and one artist comes by and paints in the sky — a sky he repeats hundreds of tim es for hundreds of paintings which arc all virtuallv identical — or as identical as humanly possible. Then another painter tonies by and paints the backgrounds

 and another does the buildings, another adds the trees and another the animals and a final one does the finishing touches. 
The paintings are then signed. While they are 
“ original” in that they are painted by hand and are not exactly alike, they are not “original” in that dozens upon dozens of almost identical paint­ ings are produced by the assembly line-method. 
They might as well be done by machine. 
We suspect there will be more and mere of these sales in San An­ tonio until the market is glutted with look-alike paintings. The painting pushers will then move on to another market. 
Local artists, of course, wish people would sup­ port them rather than some European painting 
“ laclory.” We will have to admit, however, that it is clear the people churning these paintings out have great skill. It is only unfortunate the paintings are not the products el one real ar­ tist.

Hie country last week.

Webb. 
Charge countered 

R O BB ED member Al von Armstead countered W illiam s’ charges by say­ ing, “ We don't endorse any candidate regardless of how active he has been in the community—-or inactive. 
“ We feel that the candiate should endorse what the people want." 

So saying, R O B B ED members out­ lined what they called vital needs for council attention in the district. 
Included in the list of needs were demands for sidew alks, curbing, drainage ditches and a ban on dumps near neighborhoods. 
R O B B E D m em bers told horror stories of children being killed because a lack of walkways and playgrounds forced them into the streets. 

Floodings 

They told of frequent street and pri­ vate property floodings because of in­ adequate drainage in the area. 

R O BBED then allowed each member present to tell whether or not he would support proposals tor community im­ provement. 
But the meeting was disrupted when a member of the audience was ruled out of order when he asked for time to allow members of the general public to speak. 
Council candidate Victor L. Dupree then called R O BB ED a “ dictatorship" and stormed out followed by candidate 
Effie Pope Blandin. 
After order was restored, the re­ maining five candidates we're allowed 

 time to respond to the R O B B E D de­ mands. 
The Rev. C. C. Houston said if elect­ ed. he would try to establish a repartee with the rest of council in order to win votes for District 2 projects. 
Important 
George R. Norm an called the 
R O BBED demands important, but said getting an equitable settlem ent through the courts of the city’s suit with its gas suppliers is the No. I problem facing District 2, as well as the rest of the city. 
R. G. “ Je ff" Jackson agreed that the 
R O B B E D demands were important. 
He said he would work diligently in getting needed projects approved that would benefit the district. 
Murrene Gilford said one of his ma­ jor goals would be to place the names of qualified appointees from the dis­ trict in nomination for city and civic committee posts. 
Finally, the Rev. Joe Webb, said he is in favor of all RO BB proposals and agreed to work to improve housing, drainage and the streets in the district. 
Only Jackson and Webb agreed to sign a “ social contract” to RO BBED holding them accountable to the group’s present and future demands. 

HELMUT BERGER 
. . . should recover 
Actor is in hospital 

ASSOCIATED PRESS 
ROME — Austrian movie actor 
Helmut Berger was hospitalized 
Monday after consuming a large quantity of liquor along with some medical pills, police reported. 

They said this was the finding of physicians who examined the actor, described by officers as in an agitated state after being brought to a hospital by ambulance. 

The doctors said Berger should recover in about a week. 

Edgewood center conversion voted 

By DAVID HENDRICKS 
Am id shouts of protests from teachers and mothers, the Edgewood 
School District Board of Trustees voted 
Monday evening to convert the Dr. 
Jose Cardenas Early Childhood Edu­ cation (’enter into an all-handicapped education facility. 

The action left in doubt the future of the d is tric t’s highly . acclaim ed bilingual, pre-school program for four-year-olds. 
A compromise effort by trustee 
Pablo Escamilla Jr. to keep the center open for both pre-school and handi­ capped children failed on a 3-3 vote. 
Tile tie was broken on the following motion to convert the center to a handicapped program required by the' state. Board President Dupe Vela, who did not vote on the first motion, broke the tie in favor of a handicapped center. 
Escamilla had said that with good administration the center could be utilized for both purposes until federal grants could be located for new faci­ lities for the handicapped. 
The district’s administration has failed already in seeking funds from the U.S. Department of Health, Edu­ cation and Welfare, Escam illa said, adding that HEW has about $135 mil­ 

 lion available to school districts for any purpose. 
Paren ts and concerned school teachers jammed the meeting room to protest the change recommended by 
Supt. Ruben Lopez. 
They said they wanted to keep the pre-school program as it is. They also said converting the center would iso­ late the handicapped children from healthy children their own ago. 
Keeping handicapped children with healthy students is necessary to teach both how to function later in society, teachers from the center said. 
Shift planned 
Escam illa said the pre-school pro­ gram will now be conducted in regular school buildings. 
However, he said, it may soon lose its federal funding sources because the center itself had been the biggest rea­ son for past grants. 
During the meeting, Mrs. Minnie 
Aleman, a spokesperson for teachers and concerned parents against the change, was not allowed to speak. 
At one point, Vela asked a policeman to escort M rs. Alem an out of the meeting room. However, after a short discussion she was allowed to stay in the room, but she was not allowed to speak.

SAN A N T O N IO EXPRESS— Tuesday March 15 1977 
Ban on chrome imports passes 

WASHINGTON (AIM 
An adm inistration - hacked measure rein tat­ ing a ban on U.S. imports of Rhodesian chrome 
(> ussed the House Mon­ day in what one member coiled a “ litmus test” of 
American com m itm ent o m a jo rity rule in 
Africa. 
The action, if agreed to by the Senate, would repeal the 1971 Byrd amendment under which 
U.S. companies have been able to im port 
Rhodesian chrom e de­ spite United Nations- sponsored trade embar­ goes against that nation’s white minority regime. 
The House vote was 25o to 145, with one member voting present. The Sen­ ate was expected to aet 
Tuesday on the same bill, which has the strong en­ dorsement of President 
Jimmy Carter. 
Litmus 
“ This is the litmus test of our com m itm ent to majority rule in Africa.*’ 
Rep. Stephen J. Solar/, 
D-N.Y., told the House. 
He said that if Congress did not repeal the Byre! amendment. African na­ tions would view its refusal to do so as “ an aet of national hypocrisy.” 
The bill adopted by the 
House includes an amendment by Richard lchord, D-Mo., saying that the President may suspend its provisions “ if he determines it would encourage m eaningful negotiations and further the peaceful transfer of governm ental power from m in o rity rule to m ajority rule in Rhode­ sia.” 
Proponents of the bill noted that ( arte r is scheduled to address the 
U.N. General Assembly in New York later this week and appealed for the House to adopt the measure as a symbol of 
Am erica's concern for human rights. 
Opponents 

But opponents of the ban, arguing that cutting off imports of Rhodesian chrome could make the 
United States heavily reliant on the Soviet 
Union for the material, tried to thwart the mea­ sure by amending it to apply to a ll countries which violate 
U.N. charter provisions on human rights.

Hanafi chief booked 

WASHINGTON (AIM - Hamaas 
Abdul Khaalis, leader of the Hanafi 
Moslems who held 134 people hostage last week, las hooked Monday on a charge of armed kidnaping, an offense that carries a maximum sentence of life in prison. 
“ You hate me, you hate the ground I walk on. but I don’t hate anyone,” Ab­ dul Khaalis told photographers as he left his sect's headquarters to go downtown for the b rie f booking 

 procedure. 
He and ll of his follow ers were arraigned Friday after the hostages were freed from three different places where they had been held 38 hours. 
In the negotiations thai led to the surrender, Abdul Khaalis was pro­ mised he would not be formally booked until later and that he would remain free on his own recognizance at least until a grand ju ry returns an indict­ ment.

Despite the rising Dow, 
Big Board volume only reached 19.29 m illion shares, up from 18.23 million Friday. It was the 
17th consecutive day the m arket failed to break the 2o-million mark. 
Analysts could not link the market’s upturn to a specific news d ev elo p ­ m ent but said the a d ­ vance was helped by the absence of negative economic statistics. 
“The rise seems to be co ncentrated in the 
Dow,” observed Monte 
(Jordon of Dreyfus Corp., pointing to increases in several stocks among the 
3o in the average. “ It’s focused in the av erag e rather than expressive of the whole market. 
“ To a certain extent, investors are 

 anticipating the recovery will show up in im ­ provements in “ smokes­ tack A m erica” —in the heavy in d u strie s like steel, chem icals and manufacturing,” Gordon said. 
Other analysts said the selling pressure on some of key in stitu tio n a l stocks, including Xerox, 
Polaroid, 
E a stm a n 
Kodak and Coca-Cola, had let up, allowing the market to take “ a second breath.” 
The light volume might be a reflection of inves­ tors anticipating T u e s­ d a y ’s figures on F e ­ b ru a ry 's in d u stria l production and the report expected Friday on the consumer price index.

Looking at the collec­ tions of dust on porches and autos, one finds it hard to believe that only a month ago farmers and ranchers were complain­ ing about the sw am py conditions that kept them from working their land. 
While most of the dust came from land faraw ay from South Texas, it fell on soil which has been dried out by high winds 

 ii i recent xvecks. 
Oscar Leissner. one of the brand inspectors for the Texas and Southwes­ tern Cattle Raisers Asso­ ciation, washed his w ife’s car and his truck Friday night. 
He said both looked like they had been in the 
Dust Boxx I by Saturday afternoon. 
Several visitors at the 
Union Stock Yards Mon- 
(lax rem arked that the soil still was moist a fexx inches below the surface.

The co m m issio n recom m en datio n for a tariff on foreign-made color and black-and- white television se ts would increase the aver­ age price of im p o rted color sets by $56. 
The s u g a r q u o tas would limit im ports of su g a r to 4.275 m illion tons annually for a five-year period. Sugar im ports in 1976 totaled about 4.7 million tons. 
A group of su g a r refiners opposed to the quotas contend that an annual quota of 4.2 m il­ lion tons would “ have the potential” to Increase the retail price of refin ed su g a r at least $16 per 
U.S. fam ily per y e a r, with a total impact on the economy of more than $1 billion. 

Curter now has 6o days to decide whether to ac­ cept, reject or modify the c o m m issio n ’s re c o m ­ mendations. lf he does not act, the ruling goes into effect automatically.

Association d is tric tin g plan, said M onday the cattle rustling i i i his ter­ rito ry is going on at about average pace. 
He is in c o m p le te agreement with the other inspectors’ views that the best xx ay to keep rustlers away is to brand all cat­ tle. 
Some of the cattlemen are u sin g tw o-inch brands, and these can be misread, he reported. 
He fav ors at least a four-inch brand and said a five-inch m ark is even better. The TSCR X bas a computer which can give information immediately oil brands submitted. 
“ But when the ca ttle a re not b ra n d e d , th a t computer does not work too well,’* Potts said.

M a n a gin g Editor 
President will need m ore fireside chats 

Our assessment of President 
Carter while he was still a can­ didate was that he is a tough and determ ined fellow. He made commitments during his cam ­ paign tnat were departures from many political campaigners of the past. Now, to the surprise only of those who haven’t read his biography, he is making a strong move to keep those com­ mitments. 
The Senate has renewed pre­ sidential power to reorganize the bureaucracy and the House is expected to follow suit before the end of March. 
But Mr. Carter has c h a l­ lenged the Congress where the members are sensitive — and probably least prepared to bat­ tle on merits of legislation. The 
President insists upon the right to review and oppose projects he deems to be “an inefficient use of the taxpayers’ money.’’ 
In this case, the fight is over 
19 water projects. One indepen­ dent appraisal found one of the 
19 that appears sound. The other 
18 fall into the classic definition of “pork barrel projects’’ — not economically sound but someth­ ing for the district back home that will spend federal money there and redound to the credit of sponsoring members of Con­ gress. 
Senate Majority Leader Ro­ bert Byrd said the Senate vote 
(65 to*24 last T hursday) is a 
“ clear signal to the White House that this Congress expects to be consulted on m a tters w hich come within the responsibility of the legislative branch.’’ 
He was talking about the water projects — mostly dams 
— and the P resid en t’s stated intent to review and possibly try to block them . P resid en tia l 

 press secretary Jody Powell said the President would use 
“ all lawful m eans” to cut pro­ jects once he decides they are based on “ loose criteria” and that he will seek public support for his position. 
Goal, Pow ell said, is a ba­ lanced budget within this presi­ dential term. 
. The face-off appears headed toward another fireside chat. 
The Senate is still at work on the second part of the pay-raise understanding: a financial limi­ tation and disclosure law. And another indication of how the system works, it should be noted that the so-called sunshine law-, went into effect last Saturday, amended into key ambiguities by the experts on such matters. 
There is no clear definition in the law on critical terms. The su nsh in e law is designed to force public business into public view. But there’s lack of defini­ tion for what constitu tes a meeting or agency or how public public meetings should be. 
Senate sponsor, Sen. Lawton 
Chiles, said, “ They’ve put a lot of bramblebushes and pitfalls in the path. They may try to find or invent technicalities or nit-pick­ ing points of procedure to close meetings.” 
The P resident has a m oun­ tainous task ahead. Reorganizing ing the 1,9oo agen cies of g o ­ vernment into about 2oo has a1-, ready started. It is to be a from-bottom-up process. 
We might even get an ethics law that works. 
Meetings might be more open than before. 
But Mr. Carter is taking away the jelly beans when he tries to stop those hom c-district goodies. 

Military unions not an idea 

 whose tim e has com e to Lf. S. 

Two groups whose opinions should provoke thoughtful d e­ bate stated their opposition last week to proposals to open up the military to union organization. 
.James o. Duncan, president of the 
N on-C om m issioned 
Officers Association, said “ un­ ionization of our m ilitary per­ sonnel would cause the ultimate destruction of our n ation ’s armed forces.” 
President Andy Sanchez of the American Federation of Go­ vernment Employes Local 1617 said his union opposes the idea on com petitive grounds. San­ chez saw the problem in organi­ zational rivalry for jobs. 
When the idea of union of military people cam e up, the predominant view was that it 
Id never be because it 
"L l work. Proponents keep 

 pressing their views, arguing that the military rank-and-file should have representation of their rights and privileges. 
The volunteer army has seen changes that would make many of the old-line non-coms go out of their m inds. Housekeeping and other duties aren’t done like they used to be. 
But the notion that a military fighting force could be or should be organized into unions is an idea still far ahead of its time in the ll. S. 
Non-union m ilitary should keep some of the better jobs as career-advance encouragement, however. As long as there are people running the world, we will need the military lo settle disputes and we think the m ili­ tary people should remain top quality. 

W ater hearing 

Sir: Your “Letters to the Editor” supervisor really got a lot of people in. 
Uvalde in trouble — reference to the 
.John VV. White aquifer letter (March 
9), especially the note accompanying the letter. 
In the first place, I had told my wife I was going to Austin on a chartered bus to the March 3 Water Quality Board hearing. Also. I had notified my board of directors that I was going. There had to be 25 local and area people on that bus and (‘ach had paid $9 for the round trip. 
My eyesight and hearing must be wrong, as I could have sworn that John 
White introduced me as manager of the Uvalde Chamber of Commerce and also representing the Soil and Water 
Conservation District, 
Also. I could have sworn that White also introduced the individuals present who represented the various groups who had sent in resolutions from the organizations listed. 
Tell me what to say to my w ife and board of directors when I tell them I was in Austin but your editor says none of the people representing the organi­ zations noted were present at the hearing. If you say so. I want to get our money back for the bus trip. 
Send glasses and hearing aid 
Cal Newton, 
Manager, 
Uvalde Area Chamber of Commerce 

(NOTE: Tell wife and board to rest easy. Reporter says the folks were there, just didn't testify.) 

Immunity mantle called one-sided 

Sir: Your “Gonzalez tells all” exclu­ sive by Paul Thompson and your 
“ Gonzalez doggedness” editorial prompted this letter. In the first place, the Assassination Investigation Com­ mittee is a farce and as prior investi­ gations. i.e.. Wayne Hays, Sikes and as the present Korean bribes investiga­ tions will go, the only person to pay is the taxpayer. 
The Sharptown Scandal is another example. No one but the taxpayer paid after it was all over. In fact one key figure convicted is now a judge. UBG didn t like Foreman calling him “a pinko so he went all out to see him defeated. 
In Paul Thompson's article he is quoted as calling another government employe an S.O B. The mantle of immunity is mighty one-sided, 
Leonard Dallas 
Hazard seen in TV movies 

The hazard in tuning in 
KMQL-TV “ prime time” movies is that one might assume that he had 
(she had) gone crazy. Recently, “Tail 
Gunner Joe” was filled with lies, distortions, and half-truths; a wierd job of character assassination. 
Last night (March 7), “The Wind and the Lion" was all that its title implied, but just why the beautiful widow and two children were tied in to 
“ Perdicaris” is never explained; as 

 and is. of course, unexplainable. 
Perdicaris was a Greek male (no female he) seized by Raisuli. Ar­ rangements had been made for his release, and possibly because of this, 
Teddy Roosevelt's Secretary of State 
Hay dispatched the ringing telegram to the American consul general at 
Tangier: “ Perdicaris alive or Raisuli dead.” The telegram was read at the 
Republican National Convention in 
Chicago. Perdicaris* American citi­ zenship was ruled valid by our State 
Department some time later (“ A Di­ plomatic History of the American 
People,** Thomas A. Bailey, 1958, page 
511). 
TV can be hazardous to your health, and in more ways than one. It is a powerful propaganda organ, and “Tail 
Gunner Joe” and “ Roots” are prime 

 examples of pictures that some people will consider docum entaries, fa r­ fetched as that would seem to others. 
W. M. Vestal 
Freudian slips in letter cited 

Sir: I wish to respond to Harold H. 
Graham's letter. “ Some rights must yield to society.” 
Apparently Mr. Graham disapproves of weeds on private property, which is his right, just as others have the right to disapprove of split-levels, aluminum siding, tw o-car. garages, or well- trimmed lawns. 
The Freudian slips in his letter, however, are delightfully weird. He calls for limits “on how far we can extend the rights and freedom of ow­ nership privileges.” He needn’t fear. It is impossible to extend the “ right” of a 
“ privilege.” The first is God-given, the second is a governmental favor. Pro­ perty ownership is, of course, a 
“ right.” 
Mr. Graham then calls for “ relin­ quishing” some of our “ unalienable civil rights." another howling impossi­ bility, by definition. 
I hope some of us are still around when Mr. Graham finally discovers 
- what freedom is all about. 
Egon R. Tausch 

Human rights message 
M arshall Plan for aliens 

Appoint clown and get a circus 

WASHINGTON - Well, the adage seems to hold: Appoint a clown; get a circus. 
It seems Andy Young has gone out and hired his own Andy Young. 
First some background. Six weeks ago. you will recall, our new U.N. en­ voy, Andrew Young, anointed himself 
“point man” of U.S. foreign policy. 
His bags had not yet been unpacked at the Waldorf-Astoria before Young had: 
• Dropped U.S. opposition to Han­ oi's entry into the U.N. 
• 
Pledged that Rhodesia would come running liekedy-split to the con­ ference table if only South Africa would pass the word. 
• Described Cuban military units in 
Angola as a force for “ stability and order,” who were fighting our common foe, “chaos.” 

Clarification 

Each time the Department of State or the White House would “ clarify” what it was Andy meant. 
Each time, it seemed, the long-suf­ fering Cyrus Vance would emerge be­ fore assembled reporters, his face that of the anguished and em barrassed 

Each time the Department o f State or the White House would "clarify" what it was that envoy meant. 

 neighbor who had arrived at your door to apologize personally because, yes. it was his kid who slashed the tires on your new car. 
After a brief absence from the front page, Andy last week was back out on 
“ the point.”

It’s not polite to talk about oil 

By LINDA BLANDFORD ll is not polite to talk about oil _ in Saudi Arabia, just as it w asn’t to discuss coal in the drawing rooms of Victorian England. 

The stuff comes nut of the ground in the east, where Aramcn laid the foun­ dations of American suburbia. 
It underpins the capital of Riyadh, where concrete flattens the desert. 
But for sheer wealth it’s Jeddah that counts. 
In this major commercial center on the Hod Sea a plot of land that cost ITOH two vears ago has just changed hands for $2oo,ooo. 
San Francisco 
When I heard about Jeddah in co­ op-happy Riyadh it sounded like San 
Francisco. 
“ Ah, but wait until you see Jeddah,” people would say wistfully-. 
Yes, i f s different. I f s got a few hundred thousand more people, for a start. 
Jeddah is the only city in the world that houses foreign em b assies and isn't a capital. 
The Foreign M inistry, m ore of a wedding cake than a building, over­ looks one of the best and worst views in 
Saudi — the sparkling sea that delights me after desert-bound Riyadh, and the road between, packed w it ii cars and dangerous drivers. 
Like camels 
As an Arab remarked, “ Saudis drive their cars as they drove their camels 
They look neither left nor right and they beat them until they go faster.” 
They couldn't look left or right if they wanted to: They’re blinkered by their headdresses. 
There’s twice as much traffic on the 

J clitia Ii is the only city in the world that houses fo r­ eign embassies and isn't a capital. 

 roads as there need be. Everyone’s sending notes with his driver to everyone else. 
Outings are further complicated by the fact that there are no street names or num bers and, unlike Riyadh, no visible town planning 
Directions for dinner go something like this: “ Tell the driver to go to the school. I don’t know its name; ifs the boys’ school. Take the second turn, go over a hill — but mind the oil drum s — look for a yellow gate, then count seven more. You’ll know if if s the right one, because I ’ll have a m an sta n d in g 
I here.” 
Several schools 
It turns out there are several boys’ schools in the area, the oil drum s are a mound of building rubble, the gate’s turned brown in tile sun, and every entrance has a whole crowd standing in it. 
When you've finally arrived, h ag ­ gard and neurotic, the people of Je d ­ dah make up for it by being the most generous, h o spitab le and outgoing there are anywhere. 
There is a group in Riyadh; there are groups in Je d d a h . At the very top there's only The Group. 
Ma'monn and Lita Tam er ii re part of it. They’re having a soiree. Ma’nioon owns the biggest chain of pharmacies and beauty parlors in Jeddah. 
C om pared to those still R iyadh parties with barely desegregated men and women suffering from prickly awareness of each other, this could be 
New York. 
The women arc all decked out — but in thin-strapped dresses, sometimes cut short to emphasize their chic and daring. 
The better the Jeddah name, the less flamboyant, the plainer the material, the liner the cut. 
Wealth betrayed 
Only the blue-white rocks betray the wealth in the room. None of it’s in­ sured, no one would dare burglarize the names in tins room — ifs more than their life's worth, let alone their right hand. 
The jewelry is kept at home in boxes, but it doesn t travel abroad. 
Needless to say the crim e rate in 
New York horrifies them (” l hardly dare leave our a p a rtm e n t on Fifth 
Avenue” ). 
The whisky flows unselfconsciously. 
No one is su rprised, as they are rn 
Riyadh, that I don t drink alcohol. Be­ cause if s taken so much for grunted here, it doesn't have the same obses­ sive attraction. 
In one corner I listen to some sur­ prisingly well-informed dirt-digging about who’s sleeping with whom in 
London society. But this is Jeddah, so flirtatious gossip can't stretch lo who's doing what to whom here. Not much in fact 
The Group is too small 
I’hey need each other too much 
Ruining London 
One womun held forth to m e at length on how the new rich from tile small gulf states art* ruining London 
" C lu n d g e ’s Is sill! all right; they haven’t discovered that yet, aud we alw av s go to the Dorchester. But have been to the G ro sv eno r House 
Ifs humiliating. Those gull women sit in the lobby, masked and veiled from head to foot. surrounded bv half-dressed children. But that s not the half of it. I’m told when they vc gone, the bedrooms have to be rede­ corated. They look as it the locusts have been through Those people have never used a toilet or a chair iii their lives.,,.” 
Burly Abdullah “ All” Alire/a. am ­ bassador to Washington. » holding the

In Jeddah, as nowhere else in 
Saudi Arabia, the contrast between things as they are meant to be and things as they are is great. 

It is the vast oil wealth that provides the contradictions. 

This is Fart 3 of a seven-part series excerpted from “ Super- 
Wealth.” 

 generations before that from Jeddah. 
The Alireza family was trading with 
Egypt from Arabia before the Prophet was born — and that's “ nonorigin” ? 
Nowhere else in the world is there the sam e discrepancy between' how things are meant to be (separate de­ velopment for women, no liquor, etc.) and how they are, except possibly in 
Russia. 
Now the ultim ate Saudi contradic­ tion must surely be the fact that the head of intelligence and national se­ curity affairs hasn't a drop of Saudi blood in his veins. I f s one of those more exquisite ironies that you might find hard to swallow . 
But no, if s true. 
Kamal Adham. the man who heads the intelligence system, is half Turkish and hall Albanian. 
How did he get to this ex alted position? An unbeatable combination of natural intelligence and nepotism. 
Half-brother 
Adham is the adored half-brother of 
Queen Ii fat, the adored favorite wife of 
Faisal. 
He is secretive, shadowy, moves around the world almost without dis­ turbing the air. 
To the merely curious, he is as ac­ cessible us was Howard Hughes. He travels not just on government busi­ ness; he controls an intricate financial empire and manages a hefty chunk of th** p riv a te fo rtu n e of his dead brother-in-law’s family. 
King Faisal had austere tastes and thrifty habits, he died rich. Very, very rich. 
Adham’s wells of power bear deepest down through the connection he made with Egypt when, as the most trusted of F a isa l’s servants, he was the go­ between for the country’s reconcilia­ tion with Cairo after years of feuding. 
King Sand once wasted $4 million try in g to have E g y p t’s P re sid e n t 
Nasser bumped off. 
Nasser almost certainly returned the compliment to brother Faisal. 
‘Mr. Egypt' 

Today Adham is still Saudi s “ Mr. 
E gypt,” in close contact with P re si­ dent Sadat, channeling millions to him for arm s and aid. 
Adham has an organization of his own in Saudi that can bypass official­ dom. If anyone runs into trouble in 
R iyadh or J e d d a h , K am al A dham would be the man to go to — he has the power to make decisions and the so­ phistication to weight the decisions he’s making. 
Anyone with a less than clear cons­ cience should stay away from his X-ray eyes. 
But t h e r e ’s a n o th e r A dham , “ A proper gentlem an.” as a friend puts it. 
Ile is the top of Je d d a h ’s top group 
When he is at hom e he e n te rta in s. 
There are no invitations; you’re either in or out. At home he’s a character that only Hollywood or Saudi could have created. 
I walk into his house; the next Jam es 
Bond producer could do worse than borrow this spooky, sophisticated set for a location site. 

If s the only time I feel relieved at being able to hide inside the one sm art dress from my suitcase. T here are m arble floors, white walls, beautiful carpets, chinoiserie. 
In the large, low rooms m em bers of 
The Group wander around, starting at the bar. I f s the usual crowd, caressing their whiskies, talking quietly among themselves. 
Hut all the time everyone is aware of the presence of the round, blue-eyed 
Buddha in repose in front of his video­ tape set. Adham spends most of the evening glued to it. 
Court poet 

There is a court poet here tonight, one of the finer brands. 
Kanaan (’’I’m pronounced Canaan, it flowed with milk and honey” ) a1 
Khatib was King F aisal’s homme de lettres. Noticeably, he’s one man in the room Adham’s mere presence doesn’t cut down to size. 
Is there anything this adorable old man in his 7os hasn't done? 
Al Khatib taught five British a m ­ bassado rs to the M ideast at Beirut 
University before going into the oil- exploration business. 
Ile lost seven friends in three plane crashes; he was the only survivor of one and saw some of his closest friends burned to death. 
He prefers to re m e m b e r the good days. Like the night he met Paul Getty at a cocktail party in Paris, and Getty persuaded him to be his representative in Jeddah. 
Ile quit that job w hen A risto tle 
Onassls passed by and enlisted his help. The Greek was working out an agreem ent with Saudi Arabia to con­ trol the country’s entire oil shipments. 
That deal collapsed unexpectedly; it was undermined by the CIA egged on by the worried oil companies. 
Enjoyed work 
N e v e rth e le ss, a1 K h atib enjoyed working for Onassis, nearly as much as working for Texas oil billionaire ll. L. 
Hunt. 
But while he was enjoying that job an order came from the Saudi court: 
Return and work for Faisal. He stayed by the King’s side until his death. 
Once a year he composed a poem for the Haj to be recited in Mecca, and he

Jeff team is winner 

The Jefferson High 
School Forensic Team won its 27th sweepstakes award at McMurry Col­ lege in Abilene in the 
West Texas Forensic 
League Tournament. 
Six Jefferson students qualified for the National 
Forensic Tournament in 
Seattle. Wash., later this year. 
They are Francisco 
Gonzalez, Irma Arispe, 
David Frederick. Tony

 sees dry wells 

A LIC E (A P ) - Texas oil and gas resources will vanish in about It) years, says an 
Alice- based oilman who spe­ cializes in reviving long- abandoned wells 
Within 111 years—ll at tin* most—Texas will be 
* d ry," flatly stated Bill 
Doran, a 36-year-old pe­ 

 troleum engineer and president of XRO, Inc. 
"A fte r this kind of winter, businesses will be coming to Texas. They will scramble for two dollar gas because ifs better than no gas at all and that will increase our depletion rate." 
Two dollar gas—a Do 

 ran term describing the 
$2.o2 per thousand cubic feet cost of gas on the in- trastate m arket—plus 
Doran's belief that the 
Texas wells are quickly drying up sparked the concept which prompted 
X G R ’s interest in ‘‘non- virgin gas’’ two years ago. 
‘‘Our concept is to tap partially-depleted reser­ voirs that had been abandoned for eco­ nomical reasons," ex­ plains Doran. “ This is nothing miraculous, everyone can do it hut we're zeroed in on it and have been doing it the longest.’’ 
Doran's formula is simple: Buy the rights to an abandoned well. drill through a concrete plug, install a new compressor and begin pumping away profitably. 
Doran said XRO i producing “ about a m il­ lion and a half cubic feet of gas per day” or en­ ough to warm a city of about 1o,ooo residences on a monthly basis. lh' does most of his 
‘-‘exploration” from his office desk. surrounded 
By hundreds of volumes outlining the precise location and history of more than 35,ooo South 
Texas oil and gas wells. 
“ When the price of gas got too low . it drive a lot of people out of the busi­ ness.” he said. “ Today, the $2.o2 price of intras­ tate gas has made those abandoned wells profita­ ble once again. In some instances we have to drill new wells hut once in a while, we find a well that was abandoned and all we have to do is turn on the valve. 
We have the ability to calculate remaining re­ servoirs and can justify the re-entry or condemn the prospect and we do it all from behind a desk.”

 coachee at each school as indicated by the ‘28 charter members of Co-Wi (Coaches 
Wives.) 
The them e for the wives' 

By LOIS HAASE 

A quarter of a century ago, there were ll high schools and two or three 

Lights flashed and models pranced as the I 9th annual 
Ebony Fashion Fair took over the stage and runway of 
Trinity University's 
Laurie Auditorium. 
An appreciative audience cheered fashions from ar­ ound the globe, such as this whirling 
French capesuit. The San 
Antonio stop for the traveling show benefitted the building fund of St. 
Philip’s 
Episcopal 
Church. 

Reservations may be made by contacting any coach's wife from the city's high schools. Tickets are $2 each and may also be pur­ chased at the door. You don’t have to be a member to attend. 
Twenty-five years ago, the coaches wives would sit together at athletic events during the year, thus forming the nucleus of a group that has mushroomed — just to be friends. 
For a week. once a year, when coaching schools were held in different large cities, the lack of ladies activities was apparent. In 
195o, Anna Byrd Wallace planned the first wives’ activities at the 
San Antonio coach school. In 19o1 and 19o7, the local schools were heavy with wives’ activities also 
Mavourneen Harris came up with the name, Co-Wi, which is the abbreviation of the term 
Coaches Wives. 
The basic and simple rule — to promote friendship — is still the factor for remaining a club w ith a possible m em bership of about 
IKM). 
M eetings are held once a month. 
During the year, an annual ga­ mes party is held, a family picnic in May is given and a charitable function is held in November. The past two years, the group has ta­ ken the boys from Boysville to the zoo and on a picnic.

Dear Ann; I am writing this letter in an attempt to alert parents and hope­ fully sp a re their children y e a rs of struggle, agony and heartbreak. 
I just heard a report about the low percentage of children who have had vaccinations to protect them against polio. 
I was a victim of polio in the early 
‘5os before the vaccine was developed. 
I had to undergo two operations and years of special exercises to overcome a terrible complex. 
With the help of doctors, parents and friends. I m astered most difficulties and now live a normal life. 
What happened to me was something nobody could help or prevent. But w hat would you say if someday your crip­ pled child asked you, "Why didn’t you protect me? Why didn’t you get me the vaccination?” 
Many parents arc unaw are of the d a n g e r because, th an k God, there hasn’t been a polio epidemic in a long time. It could happen again. 
Hope 
Somebody Listens 
Dear Hope: 
So do I. In the m ean­ time. oral polio vaccines are available free of charge to any school-age child in Chicago and Cook County. 
Parents who are interested should* call the Board of Health. They will be referred to the nearest clinic. 
Parents in other cities should phone their own City or County Health De­ partments for information as to what is available in their area. 

HEW Secretary 

And while we’re on the subject, hear this from Health, Education and Wel­ fare Secretary Joseph A. Califano, Jr.: 
“ Sixteen million children under 14 years of age have not been vaccinated against polio. Of course these pro­ gram s are expensive, but I will find the money to fund them .” 

Ann, 
Landers 

Current recommendations for vac­ cinating children are as follows: Polio: 
Three shots at two. four and 18 months of age. At five years — a booster. 
Diphtheria, whooping cough and te­ tanus: Protection from all three dis­ eases can be obtained in one shot. This shot should be given in four doses — the first at two months of age, again at four months, again at six months and finally at IO months. 
Measles: One shot at 15 months of age. unless an epidemic occurs, in which case the parents should consult a doctor about earlier vaccination. 
Rubella or German measles: One shot at one year of age. (This may be changed soon and given along with measles shot.) 
Mumps: One shot at one year of age. 
(May also be changed and combined with measles-rubella vaccine.) 

Are your parents too strict? Hard to reach? Ann Landers’ booklet. "Bugged 
By Parents? How to Get More F re e ­ dom.” could help you bridge the gen­ eration gap. Send 5o cents in coin with your request and a long, stam p ed , self-ad d ressed envelope to 
Ann 
Landers, Box 2171, San Antonio, Tex., 
78297.

Part III 
Collecting the New 
A ntiq ues 

 popularity of an antique is when it is reproduced. 
Several years ago this happened to the Sears. 
Roebuck turn-of-the-een- tury catalogues, followed by the 1927 catalogues. 
Even the prices on those reproductions have since gone up several dollars. 
Among the most valua­ ble catalogues are the 
193os autom obile c a ta ­ logues that are in color. 
A ntique ear collectors will pay a couple of hundred do llars for a rare one — more, if it pictures the ears they own. 
Catalogue popularity 

 runs in cycles, depending on I hi* currently popular antique or collectible; in­ terest in one stimulates in te re st in the o th er. 
(Collectors often like old maintenance catalogues for th e ir c o llectib les, since these catalogues give an idea of the parts.) 
You can be a general or specialized collector. And because of tin* scarcity of som e old ca talo g u e s, even single pages are avidly saved. 
Some of the earliest catalogues were for tool patterns, furniture, but­ tons and clocks. Thomas 
Chippendale's book of furniture designs from the 18th century has been reproduced and is most popular with antique fur­ niture collectors. 
You never know when you will find an oldie. 
Begin in your own attic or basement. Hunt for them 
(>n the shelves of old book sto re s. 
A c a talo g u e doesn’t have to be from the early 19lllls to have value; a 1922 Sears, Roe­ buck has a current price tag of from $4o-up. 
Pitfalls and Potentials ol Catalogue Collecting: 
The biggest pitfall can be the collector, who might have a tendency to collect just anything, without regard to contents, be*

 cause it is a catalogue. 
But age, rarity and pic­ torial interest are most important. 
Two of the nice things about collecting picture postcards are their low price and their wide var­ iety. Collectors may be­ gin with postcards sent from foreign countries or vacation spots.

C ategories include a n i­ m als, sports, holidays, photo cards, fairs and am usem ent parks. The more unusual the card, the more valuable it may be to trade or sell to an­ other collector.

Hitler stam p is highly collectible.

United States in 1898.

Rose Bowl Parade, the first Miss America. 

The fun is discovering som e long-forgdtten cards in an old trunk or attic. What used to cost a couple of pennies may now be a quarter or more. so shoe boxes found in attics are always worth a rummage.

Ballet equals reputation 

S A N A N T O N IO EXPRESS— Tuesdoy Morph 15 1977 

By SU SAN N A S H IE LD S th e Hartford Ballet which appeared Sunday afternoon at the Theater fur the Performing Arts lived up to its reputation as one of the country’s foremost companies. 
Plaudits go to the bril­ liant artistic director. 
Michael Uthoff, who was also choreographer for three of the four ballet numbers presented. 
His “ Brahm 's V a ria ­ tions on a Theme by 
Haydn." which opened the program , showed 
Uthoff equally at home in the traditional and the modern ballet idoms. 
Elegant dance 

An elegant court dance performed by a fine corps was followed by rom antic variations spiced with delightful surprises borrowed from modern dance. 
A highlight of the bal­ let was a pas de deux with Jeanne Tears ably 

Recital at Trinity 

Excerpts from the 
‘‘Barber of Seville" and 
‘‘The M erry W ives of 
W in d so r" w ill be fea­ tured ut a guest recital by tenor Carl Mayers and pianist Susan Ogilvy at 8 p.rn. Tuesday in Trinity 
University’s Ruth Taylor 
Concert Hall. 
The recital is free to the public. 

Review 

 partnered by Thomas 
Ciroir. Miss Tears has a soft. ly rica l quality, which enhanced the bal­ let. 

Michael Uthoff danced the role of the folk hero, Dom Dilia, in the ballet of that, name, and gave a strong and moving portrayal of the tormented man waiting the morning when he will be hanged for the murder of his m istress, Laura 
Foster. 
Nightmare 

In his nightmare, the witches beneath the gal­ lows tree change into girls of the village and the audience is carried back in time to a lively hoedown and a love duet between Tom and his sweetheart, Ann Melton. 
It ’s interrupted by the appearance of the beau­ tiful and sensual Laura. 

Laura was danced with wild abandon and sexua­ lity by Judith Gosnell, a technically strong and exciting dancer. 

Jeanne Tears brought to the role of the aristo­ 

 cratic Ann an eloquent picture of a passionate woman tortured by jea­ lousy. 

Reinforcing the dancers movements was the music of bluegrass composers, 
W illia m 
Welling and William Wa- lach. 

F a m ilia r folk tunes were accelerated in tem­ po and uncom fortably loud, as the cries of fid­ dles and bagpipes be­ come like "b la c k cats sq u allin g ." The ballet ends, as does the legend, with the audience unsure of whether the guilt of 

 murder lies with Tom or 
Ann. 
"Aves M iracles," a modern pas de deux, to the music of Lukas Foss, was performed brilliant­ ly by Kristen Corman and Curtis Mathis. Miss 
Corman has extraordin­ ary extention and a sup­ ple and expressive body. 
‘Leggieros’ 
The final offering of the afternoon, 
‘‘Leg­ gieros,” was a delightful and comical romp. Chor­ eographed by the great dance clown, Lotte Cos­ tar. to Beethoven’s “ Dia- 

 belli Variations,” it was a Dada spoof in which the company parodied the cliches of traditional bal­ let. 

The broad comedy pleased the crowd and in response to insistent applause the company graciously responded with an encore. 

Let us hope this enthu­ siastic reception w ill bring the Hartford Ballet back for future perfor­ mances. Our thanks to the Ballet Society of San 
Antonio for an exciting afternoon of ballet. 
Bakst designs at McNay 

If costumes and set de­ signs are often your main interest at a perfor­ mance, the McNay Art 
Institute has your type of exhibit. 
Starting Tuesday the institute at ROGO N. New 
Braunfels Ave. will exhi­ bit Leon Bakst’s "D e­ 

 signs For Theater. 

During his career dur-i ing the early 19oos, Bakst was hailed as the creator of 
Oriental fairylands, christened the " D e la ­ croix of Costume” and proclaimed the renovator of theatrical decoction.

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuesday March 15 1977 
Rosaiynn Carter relaxes into role 

N EW VORK TIMES SERVICE 
WASHINGTON - About a month ago, when someone asked Rosaiynn 
Carter if she thought she could wield any real power as the “ honorary chairperson” of a presidential com­ mission, she responded haltingly, he­ sitantly. a bit uncertain of her words. 
“ I think.” she said, pausing to consider them carefully, “ I think that if I want to. I can — an<J I intend to.' 
The moment passed quickly, but it may have provided a casual bench­ mark for measuring the impact of life in the White House on the President’s soft-spoken wife. 
He had been in office not quite a month that day, and the considerable weight and privilege of her own role seemed vague to her, certainly not as clearly defined as they appeared to be 
Monday when she talked at length about her new world, its possibilities, its problems and the new style sin4 has decreed for social events in the old mansion. 

“ It’s really kind of funny to me to see people kind of squirm because we don’t serve liquor in the White House,” she said in an interview in her East 
Wing office. “ I kind of enjoy it. It doesn’t bother me at all. 
“ I don’t object to anybody drinking. 
They can drink if they want to. but if I don't want to serve liquor in the White 
House. I don’t have to do it. and I don’t want to do it.” 
Personality 
Painfully shy when the President first ran for public office 15 years ago, the growth of her public personality lias matched the pace of his success. 
Similarly, her ease with the arbi­ trary power of her position seems to have increased at a rate equal to his own — neither faster nor slower, al­ most always in step. 
‘You learn from the things that you do,” she said, “ and from the things that you see." — and in the 53 days since the Carters moved into the White 
FORECAST

House, she had done and seen a great deal. 
“ You can’t believe how many things,” she said, recounting four state dinners and the press of a daily schedule that includes other public appearances and such personal pro­ jects as her honorary chairmanship of the President’s Commission on Mental 
Health and a new program for the in­ ternational exchange of American and foreign visitors. 
Still, she said, when he finishes his term — or terms — she will be quite happy to return to their rambling brick house on Woodland Drive in Plains. 
Ga., the village where they met and married nearly 31 years ago. 

Relationship 

Theirs has been a good relationship, she said, built not only on sharing and respect but on a mutual encourage­ ment for individual achievement a s well. 

For instance, she said, when he asked her to be the bookkeeper for the family’s peanut business 23 years ago, 
“ he just assumed that I could do it. So. 
I would never say, ‘I can’t do this.’ I did it, and it’s just because he had confidence in me. He thought I could do it or he knew I could do it. and so I did it. 

Senate delays vote on bill to boost interest 

ROSALYNN CARTER 
... settling in 

“ I think that's how you develop con­ fidence. because I decided that I could do those books and I got to be a good bookkeeper. I almost got my CPA li­ cense, but then Jimmy ran for gover­ nor and I stopped keeping books,” she said. 
“ We all knew that we would cam­ paign,” speaking of her three sons and herself. “ You know, .Jimmy never said, ‘I want you to go do this and go do that’ — it was just an understanding that we would ail do it.” 

EXPRESS CAPITOl BUREAU 
AUSTIN — The Senate decid­ ed to take a late lunch break 
Monday rather than take on the hot issue of hiking interest rates c h a rg e d by s m a ll loan companies in Texas. 
By an 18-12 vote, senators refused lo suspend rules so that debate could start on the Consumer Credit Reform 
Act (SB 615), sponsored by Sen. Don 
Adams of Jasper. 
The Senate will probably take up the loan rate bill Tuesday, however. 
.Adams’ bill, referred to by some as the “ loan shark hill." came up just after the Senate had wrangled way past the lunch hour with another con­ troversial measure. 
That measure was SB 185 by Sen. 
Max Sherman of Amarillo. It passed on a voice vote and was sent to the House. 
Eminent domain 
Sherman’s hill would grant the power of eminent domain to builders of a proposed coal slurry pipeline from 
Texas to western coal states. 
Sherman said Texas will need all the 
'alternative forms of energy transpor­ tation it can get in the future to fire electric generating plants. 
Bringing a coal solution in through slurry pipelines would also help insure competition with the railroads for freight rates in hauling coal. Sherman

Tho University of Texas at San 
Antonio has been granted full accreditation. 
UTSA President Dr. Peter T. Flawn made the announcement Monday after he received notification from the 
Southern Assn. of Colleges and 
Schools. 
“ We are very pleased with this ex­ pression of confidence in our academic programs, faculty, students and ad­ ministration,” ho said. 
Accredited at the graduate level iii 
1674. the new university became eligi­ ble for undergraduate accreditation when it awarded its first bachelor's degrees in May 1976. 
A SACS team visited UTSA last 
October and the organization's dele­ gate assembly voted in December to grant full accreditation. 
The report of the accreditation visit­ ing team, chaired by Dr. William Ba­ 

 yard. Virginia Polytechnic Institute and State University, noted UTSA has faced the challenge of organizing a large multipurpose university. 

“ In a very few years, indeed, the university, acting under imaginative and dedicated leadership, organized a substantial graduate program . . . and opened its programs to several thou­ sand undergraduate st inputs at all le­ vels of study in one fell swoop,” the committee observed. 
The report cited “the recruitment of a diversified, highly qualified, cooper­ ative and energetic faculty’’ as an 
“ impressive achievement.” The team also commended the faculty for their strong sense of dedication in meeting extra demands and said the faculty 
“ set high standards for itself in teach­ ing and research.” 
Dr. Flawn noted because accredita­ tion applies retroactively within the 

 year awarded, all UTSA graduates hold degrees from an accredited uni­ versity. 

M in n e s o t a m i g h t 

 e x e m p t S q u a r e s 

ST. PAUL. Minn. (A P) - The Slate of Minnesota is going to be out some dough from those who do-si-do. 
The state Senate gave preliminary approval Monday to a bill exempting admissions to square dances from the 
Minnesota sales tax. 
Sponsor Wayne Olhoft said the bill will cost the state $3.(MKI a year or less in revenue but may save more than that in administrative costs. 
The 4 per cent sales tax still would apply to other types of dances. 
“ Only us squares would be exempt,” 
Olhoft said. 

 added. 
San Antonio was mentioned several times as an example of one city which has had problems negotiating a firm freight rate with railroads to haul coal needed for its power plants. 
Sen. Glenn Rot Itmann of San Antonio was the only member of the Bexar 
County Senate delegation to vote against Sherman’s bill. 
Not convinced 
Since he voted against the hill in committee. Kothmann says he has not been convinced a coal slurry pipeline from Colorado to the Houston area will lower railroad freight rates for San 
Antonio. 
Sens. Frank Lombardino ot San An­ tonio. and John Traeger of Seguin, supported the Sherman bill. 
Adams’ small loans interest bill could prove to he one of the hottest issues debated in the Senate since the 
Legislature convened in January. 
The bill would provide for increased interest rates over a new bracket of small loans. 
They would range from an sis charge per $1oo on loans of up to $3oo. a 
812 charge per $1oo for loans between 
$3oo and $1,2oo; and an $8 charge per 
$1oo for loans over $1,2oo. 
The add on charges figure out to an­ nual interest rates ranging from 14 per cent to 3o per cent. 
Set future rates 
Adams' hill also includes a provision which would set future interest rates based on the cost of making loans and the rate of inflation. 
“ The even higher rates proposed by 
SBO 15 will lead to more defaults and more punishment of the people of 
Texas." said the bill’s leading adver­ sary. Sen. Bill Batman of Ganado. 
Batman said he is prepared to fili­ buster against-Adams’ bill long enough to draw the public’s attention to its excesses. 
The Bexar delegation again ap­ peared divided on this issue. Sens. 
Lombardino and Kothmann. voted in favor of suspending the rules so the loan bill could lie brought up for de­ bate. 
Traeger voted against suspension.

T o o bad WTA; 
R en ee will play 

Win or lose, Dr. Renee Ri­ chards should enjoy competing in the $2o,ooo Lionel-McFarlin 
Cup tennis tournam ent here next week. 

She d oesn 't have to w orry about finding a spot in the field. She won t be barred. There will be no boycott by the other players, no organized attempt to keep her off the court. 
Not everywhere is Richards treated so nicely. 
In fact, the W omen’s Tennis Asso­ ciation (WTA) has issued a "irm, flat ruling that proh ib its Dr. R ic h a rd s from playing in their events. 

Most WTA members are the biggies^ in wom en’s tennis, the stars of the 
Virginia Slims circuit and World Team 
Tennis competition. 

They argue that Dr. Richards is not eligible to compete against the other ladies because Renee was not always a w om an. Previously a m ale doctor, 
R enee b ecam e an in te rn a tio n a lly known te n n is p la y e r a fte r a sex change. 
The WTA ignores the fact that Dr. 
Richards is legally a female. 
Julie Heldman, formerly a Virginia 
Slims player, says the WTA refuses to allow Dr. Richards to compete because 
“ they are afraid. 
“ They’re scared. They don’t know what’s happening,” Julie said during a visit yesterday. 
J u l i e ’s m o th e r. G la d y s, is the founder of the Lionel Cup tour, which consists of four different $2o.o(1o tour­ naments. beginning with the one here next Monday through Saturday (quali­ fying starts tomorrow). 

“ T hat is one of the g re a t things about the Lionel Cup,” explains Held­ man. "F or a $25 entry fee, any woman can play, with no exceptions.” 
Some of the players in tile field of 32 will be WTA m e m b ers. Obviously, their association will not appreciate them entering the same tournament as 
Renee Richards. 
“ It’s sad, it really is sad,” Heldman says, obviously not agreeing with the* way the WTA is handling the Richards case. 
Anyway, Dr. Richards will not have to worry about picket lines or boycotts here next week. She is one of 24 women who have been guaranteed spots in the field of 32 for next Monday. 

Spur exposure 

Have you noticed the attention San 
Antonio has been receiving in recent weeks? 
First. ABC television announced that its Wide World of Sports would send lovable Howard Cosell to our dear city on March 27 for national exposure of a quarterfinal round of the I .S. Boxing 
Tournament of Championships. 
A few days later, un independent company in New York wanted to carry a card in April live from S A. on a 
Friday night. That deal didn’t develop because of a conflict with Fiesta festi­ vities. 

More recently, in an exclusive story here Saturday, we learned that the 
C S. Boxing Tournament of Champions wanted to stage its 9o-minute, nation­ ally televised semifinal card here on 
April 2 — covered from beginning to end by Cosell and his Wide World of 
Sports buddies. 

All of a sudden, there were three opportunities for nationally televised events in San Antonio. Have you won­ dered why we received this load of attention? 

It was almost as if the rest of the world was slapped in the face and told, 
“ Go to San Antonio. It is a wide open market, a good sports town.” 

For years and years this city s u r­ vived without a single nationally tele­ vised event. Maybe it s purely coinci­ dental that we got three offers within a two-week period. 
But I don’t think so. 

This recognition is a direct result of the Spurs being such big hits on CBS’ regional coverage of NBA games tilts season. 
At least, that s the way I fi­ gure it. 

On seven occasions fans in various parts of the I .S. have seen the run- n in g -an d -g u n n in g S p u rs on CBS. 
Somebody must lie impressed because only one club (Chilly) in the 22-team league has l>een on ( BS more than S A 

Running helps 

And if >ou happen to be wondering why the Spurs are so popular on CBS, I again have a theory. 

The main thing TY networks fear is a lopsided, boring event. They blush, frown and c u rs e at c o n te sts that quickly become one-sided yawners. 

Obviously, pro basketball will al­ ways have a lot ai lopsided contests but 
CHH can protect itself by going with the teams that produce constant ex­ citement, no mailer what the score is. 

By JIM O’BRIEN 
NEW YORK — A big change has come over Larry Kenon in the two seasons he’s spent in 
San Antonio. 

Now he has the sam e kind of confi­ dence off the basketball court he’s al­ ways had on it. He knows more than ever that the Nets blew it when they traded him. And he says so, without hesitation. He’s,not shy anymore. 
“ I felt they didn't appreciate my ta­ lent,” said Kenon. “ Otherwise, they would have nev er even thought of trading me. 

“ I had a no-trade clause in my con­ tract, and I didn’t have to go. But just the thought of them wanting to trade m e ....th e r e was no way I d have stayed.” 
(The Spurs have defeated the Nets twice in three meetings this season. 
Kenon has been the leading scorer in the series with 84 points, including 39 in the last gam e.) 
It has to gnaw at the Nets that they are still paying part of Kenon’s salary while he’s coming back to beat them. 
Kenon and G eorge G ervin began their pro careers as sidekicks to Julius 
Erving, but both have emerged to de­ m o n stra te spectacular skills and gain 

 personal recognition. 
Gervin broke in with the Virginia 
Squires when they employed Erving the year before he came to the Nets. 
The Spurs have a fun team to watch, and Kenon and Gervin are two of the main ingredients. They’re both long 

Jim O'Brien is the NBA beat reporter for the New 
York Post. 

 and lean, can leap into the rafters, and have a lot of showboat in their sleek bodies. 
“ It was tough for me to play with Dr. 

J ." said Kenon. “ I couldn’t take full advantage of my talent. It was the best that we parted company Now’ I can bring the ball up the floor once in a while and create things myself. I don’t think I'd e v e r have re a c h e d my potential with that team .” 
Even so, he was satisfied for the most part while he was playing for the 
Nets. He and his best buddy, John 
Williamson, were scorers at heart, hut they sacrificed some of their game for the good of Dr. J and the team. 
Ken»m and W illiam son sta rte d as rookies in the 1973-74 season and made a huge contribution to a championship effort. Kevin Loughery always raved 

 about their competitiveness. 
Kenon and Dr. J were the forwards, 
Billy F au lt/ was in the pivot, and Wil­ liamson and Brian Taylor were in the b a c k c o u rt. M ike G ale and the late 
Wendell Ladner were valued reserves. 
“ It was a dream team ,” says Kenon now. In their second season together. they had a better record than they did the year before, but they faltered near the end aqd fell apart in the playoffs. 
T hey 
W e r e k no cked out of the playoffs in five gam es by the Spirits of 
St. Louis, a team they’d beaten in all ll regular-season meetings. After that.

Spurs p la y Nets 

By JEFE GROSSMAN 
O f THE EXPRESS STAff 
Never let it be said the Spurs turn down freebies. 
Other teams have paid dearly because of the ’Spurs’ profi­ ciency at the free throw line. 

San Antonio closes its four-game ho- m estand T uesday against the New 
York Nets at the Convention Center 
Arena. Tipoff is 7:36 p.m. 
While the Spurs are third in the NBA in number of attem pts from the free throw line, and second in percentage, they are first in productivity. 
Free throws have accounted for an average of 25.1 of the Spurs’ points this 

 season. How many gam es have they won at the line? 
The Spurs visit the foul line s o fre­ quently because of their reliance on the inside game. And inside is where the large majority of shooting fouls are called. 
" I ’m sure we’ve won four or five that way, maybe more," says Spurs Coach 

The Spurs will hold their annual 
Photo Night prior to the Nets game. 
Players will be available for picture taking starting at 6 p.m. 

Doug Moe. “ We’re fortunate to have a good free throw-shooting team ." 
D enver (33.8) and P h ila d e lp h ia 
(33.4) average more trips to the line 

 than the Spurs (31.3), and Kansas City shoots a b ette r p e rcen tag e (.81o to 
.8o1). 

The Nuggets gain an average of 24.7 points a the line, while the Sixers get 
24.6. The Kings score only 2o.9 because they average just 26 trips to the line per game. 

“ In order for us to get a lot of free throws, we’ve got to get up and down the court quickly,” says Moe. “ That doesn’t give the defense time to get set."

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuej<joy Morch 15 1977 
Woes haunt 
Philadelphia 

PH ILA D ELPH IA (AP) - The ta- ent-laden Philadelphia 76ers were 
(opposed to march right to the Na* ional Basketball Association cham­ pionship. Woes. however, keep raining til their parade. 
The boiling pot of discontent bubbled igain Sunday when the Tilers lost to the 
"hicago Bulls at borne. They're still in irst place in the Atlantic Division with i J9-27 record, but the harmony us lost. 
Guard Lloyd Free wants to get out. 
Julius Erving’s wife has become a 
Tit ic and Coach Gene Shoe has spread himself thin trying to keep the lid on. 
‘H don’t want to play any more." said Free, a 6-foot-2 guard whose playing time has been fluctuating. 
On Friday, he pumped in 25 points in he 54 minutes he played in a victory over Seattle. On Sunday, he played 13 minutes and scored only four points. 
"I'm tired of being insulted." Free 
^aid. referring to Shoe. "He's taken my entire game away. Playing me. 
Sitting me. Playing me. I ain’t no yo­ yo. 
"There’s a lot of teams out there that want me. Trade me and 1 11 be hap­ pier." he said. 
Before Free’s diatribe. Dr. .Cs wife. 
Turquoise, took a swipe at the 7Bers in 

 a copyrighted article in the New York 
Times. 
"They’re in first place because of their talent. They’re not there because they’re playing Ilk*' a team." she said. 

"You think they’re out there trying to win? Sure, some want to win. And some don’t. A team, is supposed to be like a family. And this team is playing like individuals." she added. 

Dealing with unhappy players is hardly the thing Shoe needs while preparing for the playoffs. 

"It’s not an ideal situation. But I’m prepared to accept the problems provided we win." he said. 
"Ideally, the makeup of our team should include eight players who are reasonably talented. Then, you fill in the nine to 12 spots with guys who are happy to be here provided they have a role and can help win certain games,” 
Shoe added. 

"I can find no fault with a player if lie s unhappy because he’s not playing more." he said, taking the philosophi­ cal approach. 
The situation is really taking away from the team.

Kevin Loughery lust faith starting five. 
Dave DeBusschere, who took over as general manager at the start of that season, had never been a big fan of 
Kenon. ll was thought that he didn’t complement Dr. J. that the Nets need­ ed a bruiser, somebody who could re­ bound, play defense and muscle people under the boards. 
So, in separate deals, they sent Ken- on and Gale, a solid two-way guard, to the Spurs, and got Swell Nater and some money in return. Later Paultz was dispatched to San Antonio for Rich 
Jones, Kim Hughes, ( huck Terry and 
Bobby Warren Warren was then sent to San Diego for Tim Bassett in a steal. 

The Nets came back to win the ABA title that season and have always de­ fended their trades by pointing to the trophy. “ We d have won it again our­ selves, and it would have been more of a team thing. We’d still be a threat to take it all in this league, too. We were that good. 
"Don’t tell me what Rich Jones did for them. Doc carried all of them. Rich 
Jones and the rest of them. Doc car­ ried them all year. They rode on his back. And they wouldn’t have beaten us in the playoffs, except that the ref and injuries killed us. 
"Look. Jones was on his way out of pro ball when he got to New York. Now he’s gone for good." 
Worse yet. so is the Nets’ dream team.

Qualifying for the Lionel-McFarlin 
Cup tennis tournament begins 
Wednesday at McFariin Tennis 
Center. 
The championship flight of the tour­ nament will have a field of 32. with action starting next Monday and run­ ning through finals on Saturday. 
Qualifying is scheduled this week on 
Wednesday and Thursday. The eight quarterfinulists advance to prelimin­ ary qualifying Sunday, with those eight winners joining 24 others for the start of Monday’s tournament. 
There is no admission fee for quali­ fying matches. Tickets next Monday and Tuesday are 12, $3 for Wednesday and Thursday and $4 for Friday and 
Saturday. 
Ducats are available at Joske’s. the 
Arena box office, Jam and Jelly, Ten­ nis Anyone and Tennis Woman.

Dear Dr. Thosteson: My elderly aunt fell and broke her hip. We were surprised when the doctor said the fall didn't break the hip. 
Fie said it was already broken and that caused her to fall. Can you explain? — R. 
I can’t comment on your aunt's case, specifically. 
However, many falls occur because there has been damage already done to the bone structure. A com­ mon cause is bone-thinning (osteoporosis), which can come after 

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuesday March 15 1977 

9 p.m.: NBC NEWS REPORTS looks into the oil tanker business, particularly the sinking of the Argo Merchant, which caused the nation’s largest oil spill last 
November. Circumstances are investigated and ship’s officers are interviewed. 
John Dancy reports. (Ch. 4)

 menopause with the loss of natural hormones in the body. It is possible that this is what the doctor deferred to, al­ though Paget's disease of the bone may also cause fractures. 
Of course, a serious fall can worsen an al­ ready bad situation. 
This is speculation. No need to quibble. What is important is to treat os­ teoporosis by trying to control the bone clacium loss. 
Y east infections 

Dear Dr. Thostesdn: 
Lately, I ’ve been p la­ gued by recurrent yeast infections. I have been using 
Mycostatin vaginal tablets. Can the yeast remain in vour underclothing alter washing in soap and water? 
Would switching to a less strong birth control pill or eliminating it altogether prevent toe from getting this irritating infection? — Mrs. J.K . 

The Mycostatin (mystatin) tablet contains an an­ tibiotic that acts against the yeast (a fungus). Nor­ mally, improvement is noted in from two to ihrec^ days. Are you sure you are insetting thj tablet" correctly? It should be placed high in the vagina for hest, effects. Follow the instruction on the package* carefully. It will explain proper use of the applica­ tor. 
The birth control pill can be a factor in the per­ sistence of the infection. Try another method of contraception for a while. The yeast organism would not remain on the underclothing to cause reinfec­ tion This is often a tricky business. 
Sometimes prolonged use of oral antibiotics themselves will alter the mucous membrane of the vagina and allow growth of yeast organisms. My booklet on vaginitis may give von other leads (25 cents and a stamped, self-addressed envelope to.me in care of this newspaper). 

W rinkles 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: I d like to know if wrinkles in the ear lobe are a clue to some kind of trouble. I recall reading somewhere where it could indicate high blood pressure — Mr.s. J.G . 

I know of no evidence linking ear lobe wrinkles to high blood pressure or any other disease. This theory surfaced a year or so ago. Medical myths die hard and this one should be around for a long while. 

' You Can Stop Sinus Trouble!" is the title of Dr. Thosteson s booklet, explaining what sinus trouble really is and what can be done about it for a copy write to him in care of this newspaper, enclosing a long, self-addressed, stamped envelope and 35 cents.

Luciano Pavarotti in Puccini's masterpiece of youth, love and death. Set in Paris' Latin Quarter in the 183o's, “ La Boheme" is a romantic story of life in the Bohemian artist community. 

1o:1o BLITHE SPIRIT 

The 1945 film version of Noel Coward's stage play, one of the classic comedies of contemporary theatre, stars 
Rex 
Harrison as a novelist who finds married life with his second wife (Constance 
Cummings) complicated by the appear­ ance of his first wife's ghost (Kay Ham­ mond). A most eccentric medium, played by Margaret Rutherford, has started the trouble during an unfortunate seance.

Yugoslav critic praises Carter 

 w a s h i n g t o n p o s t s e r v ic e 
BELGRADE - Pre sident Carter's outspo­ ken support of human rights around the world has had “ a great, posi­ tive ech o 
In the cou n tries of E astern 
Europe." 

The speaker is M in ­ yan Djilas, a post-war leader of the Yugoslav 
Communist party who for more than 2o years has also been its best known and m ost out­ spoken critic. 

“ It is something like the beginning of Amer­ ica, acting once again like her tra d itio n s. 
Carter cannot change xJhe situation in Eastern 

Milovan Djilas 

Europe, but maybe he can influence govern ­ ments to be more care­ ful. more respectful of laws," he said. 

Y et, 
D jila s sa id , t arter m ust proceed with utmost caution. Ile warns that attacking 

 the 
S oviet s y ste m head-on^ is futile and that the Soviets also cannot be 
“ b la c k ­ m a ile d " into c o n c e s ­ sions on human rights through trade restric­ tions . 

“ What I am afraid of, if a n y th in g , is that 
American pragmatism, in the form of the mul­ tinational corporations, will not follow Carter's ideals. Profits are im ­ portant, hut in our time there is more to survi­ val.’’
